O-RAN Fronthaul Working Group
Control, User and Synchronization Plane Specification
This is a re-published version of the attached final specification.
For this re-published version, the prior versions of the IPR Policy will apply, except that the previous
requirement for Adopters (as defined in the earlier IPR Policy) to agree to an O-RAN Adopter License
Agreement to access and use Final Specifications shall no longer apply or be required for these Final
Specifications after 1st July 2022.
The copying or incorporation into any other work of part or all of the material available in this
specification in any form without the prior written permission of O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.  is prohibited,
save that you may print or download extracts of the material on this site for your personal use, or copy
the material on this site for the purpose of sending to individual third parties for their information provided
that you acknowledge O-RAN ALLIANCE as the source of the material and that you inform the third
party that these conditions apply to them and that they must comply with them.

discussions at the June 27 meeting.  Most changes are related to mandatory/optional

0003.

mandatory / optional modifications, as well as adding Section Extention =6 and =7.

Chapter 1 Introductory Material 1
1.1 Scope 2
This Technical Specification has been produced by the O-RAN Alliance. 3
The contents of the present document are subject to continuing work within O-RAN and may change following formal 4
O-RAN approval. Should the O-RAN Alliance modify the contents of the present document, it will be re-released by O-5
RAN with an identifying change of release date and an increase in version number as follows:  6
Release x.y.z 7
where: 8
x the first digit is incremented for all changes of substance, i.e. technical enhancements, corrections, updates, 9
etc. (the initial approved document will have x=01). 10
y the second digit is incremented when editorial only changes have been incorporated in the document.  11
z the third digit included only in working versions of the document indicating incremental changes during the 12
editing process. 13
The present document specifies the control plane, user plane and synchronization plane protocols used over the 14
fronthaul interface linking the O-DU (O-RAN Distributed Unit) with the O-RU (O-RAN Radio Unit) with a Lower 15
Layer Functional Split-7-2x based architecture (explained below).  The scope of this document includes both LTE and 16
NR (5G).  A separate document contains the O-RAN M-Plane (management plane) specification. 17
In the following, “Layer 1” and “Physical Layer” are assumed to be synonymous. 18
In the main body of this specification (in any “chapter”) the information contained therein is normative meaning 19
binding on any compliant system, unless explicitly described as informative (a cap ability described as “optional” may 20
or may not be included in a compliant system but if it is included it must comply with the optional capability 21
description).  Information contained in an “Annex” to this specification is always informative.  22
1.2 References 23
The following documents contain provisions which, through reference in this text, constitute provisions of the present 24
document. 25
- References are either specific (identified by date of publication, edition number, version number, etc.) or 26
non-specific. 27
- For a specific reference, subsequent revisions do not apply. 28
- For a non-specific reference, the latest version applies. In the case of a reference to a 3GPP document (including 29
a GSM document), a non-specific reference implicitly refers to the latest version of that document in Release 15. 30

1.3 Definitions and Abbreviations and Conventions 15
1.3.1 Definitions 16
For the purposes of the present document, the terms and definitions given in 3GPP TR 21.905 [1] and the following 17
apply. A term defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same term, if any, in 3GPP 18
TR 21.905 [1]. 19
BMCA: Best Master Clock Algorithm  20
C-Plane: Control Plane: refers specifically to real-time control between O-DU and O-RU, and should not be confused 21
with the UE’s control plane 22
c_eAxC: component eAxC: a portion of an eAxC flow assigned to a specific O-DU processing element.    Includes the 23
fields BandSector_ID, CC_ID, RU_Port_ID and DU_Port_ID (see sections 3.1.3.1.6 and 3.1.3.2.4) 24
CA: Carrier Aggregation  25
Cascade mode: Mode of Shared cell which is realized by several O-RUs cascaded in chain. (See Chapter 11) 26
DL: DownLink: data flow towards the radiating antenna (generally on the LLS interface) 27
eAxC: extended Antenna-Carrier: a data flow for a single antenna (or spatial stream) for a single carrier in a single 28
sector.  Includes the fields BandSector_ID, CC_ID and RU_Port_ID but not DU_Port_ID (see sections 3.1.3.1.6 and 29
3.1.3.2.4) 30
eEEC: enhanced Ethernet Equipment Clock  31
ePRTC: enhanced Primary Reference Time Clock 32
FFO: Fractional Frequency Offset.  This is defined as ∆f/f_norm which is used to describe frequency error, typically on 33
the output of the T-TSC filter in the O-RU. It is the same as the Fractional Frequency Deviation defined in ITU -T 34
G.810. 35
FHM mode: Mode of Shared cell which is realized by FHM and several O-RUs. (See Chapter 11) 36
Hop: physical link between 2 s-plane nodes (where node can be O-DU, switch or O-RU) as defined in IEEE 802.1CM 37
LAA: Licensed-assisted access: Carrier aggregation with at least one secondary cell operating in the unlicensed 38
spectrum. 39
LLS: Lower Layer Split: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer (intra-PHY based) 40
functional split. 41
LLS-U: Lower Layer Split User-plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer functional 42
split. 43
LLS-C: Lower Layer Split Control-plane: logical interface between O-DU and O-RU when using a lower layer 44
functional split. 45

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        13
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
High-PHY: those portions of the PHY processing on the O-DU side of the fronthaul interface, including FEC 1
encode/decode, scrambling, and modulation/demodulation. 2
Low-PHY: those portions of the PHY processing on the O-RU side of the fronthaul interface, including FFT/iFFT, 3
digital beamforming, and PRACH extraction and filtering. 4
M-Plane: Management Plane: refers to non-real-time management operations between the O-DU and the O-RU 5
O-CU: O-RAN Control Unit – a logical node hosting PDCP, RRC, SDAP and other control functions 6
O-DU: O-RAN Distributed Unit: a logical node hosting RLC/MAC/High-PHY layers based on a lower layer functional 7
split. 8
O-RU: O-RAN Radio Unit: a logical node hosting Low-PHY layer and RF processing based on a lower layer functional 9
split.  This is similar to 3GPP’s “TRP” or “RRH” but more specific in including the Low-PHY layer (FFT/iFFT, 10
PRACH extraction).    11
OTA: Over the Air 12
OTDOA: Observed Time Delay Of Arrival 13
PRTC: Primary Reference Time Clock 14
PTP: Precision Time Protocol 15
Q<I.F>: denotes a signed two’s-complement I+F bit fixed point number with I signed integer bits, and F fractional bits.  16
S-Plane: Synchronization Plane: refers to traffic between the O-RU or O-DU to a synchronization controller which is 17
generally an IEEE 1588 Grand Master (however, Grand Master functionality may be embedded in the O-DU). 18
Shared cell: The operation for the same cell by several O-RUs. (See Chapter 11) 19
Slot: this is a group of 14 symbols, for LTE and NR.  LTE has a separate slot definition within 3GPP which is 7 20
symbols but that definition is not used in this specification.  So for NR, “slot” in this document means slot as 3GPP 21
defines it, but for LTE “slot” in this document correlates to the LTE “TTI” as defined by 3GPP. 22
Spatial stream: the data flow on the DL associated with precoded data (may be same as layers or different if there is 23
expansion in the precoding), and on UL associated with the number of outputs from the digital beamforming 24
(sometimes called “beams”).  25
SSM: Synchronization Status Message: part of ITU G.781 and G.8264 standards. 26
SYNCE: Synchronous Ethernet 27
T-BC: Telecom Boundary Clock 28
T-GM: Telecom Grand Master 29
T-TSC: Telecom Slave Clock  30
TAE: Time Alignment Error as defined by 3GPP TS36.104 and TS38.104 31
TE: Time Error as defined by eCPRI network requirement specification  32
TRX: Refers to the specific processing chain in an O-RU associated with D/A or A/D converters.  Due to digital 33
beamforming the number of TRXs may exceed the number of spatial streams, and due to analog beamforming the 34
number of TRXs may be lower than the number of antenna elements. 35
U-Plane: User Plane: refers to IQ sample data transferred between O-DU and O-RU 36
UL: UpLink : data flow away from the radiating antenna (generally on the LLS interface) 37
UNI: User Network Interface as defined by eCPRI network requirement specification  38
UQ<I.F>: denotes an unsigned I+F bit fixed point number with I unsigned integer bits and F fractional bits 39

1.3.3 Document Conventions 10
Terminology: “ignored” 11
Within this specification, it is sometimes stated that certain information elements are to be “ignored” by the receiver 12
(generally for “reserved” fields but in some cases other fields too).  In this case, the fields shall  be ignored for the 13
purposes of processing at the O-RAN application level, but in the case of certain packet error-checking such as 14
Ethernet-layer CRC or parity calculations, the fields shall be included in the CRC or parity calculations.  As well, in the 15
event of packet encryption, the “ignored” fields shall be encrypted along with the other packet payload information.  16
1.3.3.1 Fields and Bitmasks in Messages 17
This document describes the format of several messages and data structures within messages.  18
Notation “X[k]” represents kth bit in a field X. Note bit X[0] is the least significant bit of field X. Where applicable, a 19
sequence of bits in a field X can be interpreted as an unsigned integer value 𝑋𝑣𝑎𝑙 calculated with formula: 20
𝑋𝑣𝑎𝑙 =∑2^𝑋[𝑘]
𝑁−1
𝑘=0
 21
where N is number of bits in field X. 22
Notation “X[a:b]” represents a sequence of bits in field X starting from bit X[a] and ending at bit X[b] inclusive.  23
The tables describing formats of messages and data structures use a notation of which an example is presented in  Table 24
1-1.  25
Table 1-1 Example of table presenting format of a data structure 26
0 (msb) 1 2 3 4 5 6 7 (lsb) # of
bytes
octet
Y[3:0] X[11:8] 1 N
X[7:0] 1 N+1
 27
The example data structure presented in Table 1-1 is interpreted as follows: 28
• Field Y has 4 bits. Bits Y[3] to Y[0] of field Y are in octet N; Y[3] - the most significant bit of field Y is in the 29
most significant bit of octet N. 30
• Field X has 12 bits. Bits X[11] to X[8] of field X are in octet N; X[8] - is in the least significant bit of octet N.  31
Bits X[7] to X[0] of field X are in octet N+1; X[7] - is in the most significant bit of octet N+1 and X[0] is in 32
the least significant bit of octet N+1. 33
This corresponds to a structure that maps every bit as presented in Table 1-2 below. 34

Chapter 2 Architecture & Requirements 1
Architectural aspects 2
The architecture of eNB or gNB with O-DU and O-RUs is shown in Figure 2-1. LLS-C and LLS-U provide C-plane 3
and U-plane over LLS interface, respectively. 4
In this architecture, O-DU and O-RU can be defined as follows. 5
Lower Layer Split Central Unit (O-DU): a logical node that includes the eNB/gNB functions as listed in section 2.1 6
split option 7-2x, excepting those functions allocated exclusively to the O-RU. The O-DU controls the operation of O-7
RUs. 8
O-RAN Radio Unit (O-RU): a logical node that includes a subset of the eNB/gNB functions as listed in section 2.1 9
split option 7-2x. The real-time aspects of control & user plane communication with the O-RU are controlled by the O-10
DU. 11
O-DU
LLS-C
O-RU O-RU

LLS-C LLS-U
eNB/gNB
...
 12
Figure 2-1 : eNB/gNB architecture with O-DU and O-RUs 13
 14
2.1 Functional Split 15
When considering the functional split defining a fronthaul interface there are two competing interests: 16
a) There is a benefit in keeping an O-RU as simple as possible because size, weight, and power draw are primary 17
deciding considerations and the more complex an O-RU, the larger, heavier and more power-hungry the O-RU 18
tends to be; 19
b) There is a benefit in having the interface at a higher level which tends to reduce the interface throughput 20
relative to a lower-level interface – but the higher-level the interface, the more complex the O-RU tends to be. 21
To resolve this conundrum, O-RAN has selected a single split point, known as “7-2x” but allows a variation, with the 22
precoding function to be located either “above” the interface in the O-DU or “below” the interface in the O-RU.  For the 23
most part the interface is not affected by this decision, but there are some impacts namely to provide the necessary 24
information to the O-RU to execute the precoding operation.  O-RUs within which the precoding is not done (therefore 25
of lower complexity) are called “Category A” O-RUs while O-RUs within which the precoding is done are called 26
“Category B” O-RUs.  See Figure 2-2 for a depiction of this dual O-RU concept. 27

The inclusion of these two O-RU categories has certain implications for the LTE and NR functional splits in both DL 3
and UL which are reflected in the following sections.  In particular, for a Category B O-RU to implement precoding for 4
LTE TM2-TM4 some special C-Plane instructions need to be provided to the O-RU from the O-DU; this is described in 5
a later section of this document.  For LTE TM5-10 and NR, no special instructions are needed because the precoding 6
may be included in a digital beamforming processing block within the O-RU for a Category B O-RU (even for analog 7
beamforming O-RUs), while for a Category A O-RU, the precoding would be executed in the O-DU and any 8
beamforming in the O-RU, if present, would exclude the precoding calculation. 9

Split 7-2x 12
Description: 13
In the DL, OFDM phase compensation [3GPP TS38.211 clause 5.4], iFFT, CP addition, and digital beamforming 14
functions reside in the O-RU as well as precoding for Category B O-RUs. The rest of the PHY functions including 15
resource element mapping, precoding, layer mapping, modulation, scrambling, rate matching and coding res ide in the 16
O-DU.  Precoding must be in the O-DU for Category A O-RU support but this only applies if the number of precoder 17
output spatial streams is 8 or less, otherwise precoding must be in the (Category B) O-RU. 18
The option of including some PHY functionality in a radio unit was not included in 3GPP TR 38.801 Rel 14 study. 19
Benefits and Justification: 20
O-RAN FH
O-RAN FH

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        20
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
- Interface simplicity: Transfer of user plane data is based on Resource Elements / Physical Resource Blocks, which 1
simplifies the data mapping and limits the required associated control messages 2
- Transport Bandwidth Scalability: Lower split options (e.g., splits 7-1 and 8) scale based on number of antennas. In 3
contrast, 7-2x interface scales based on “streams”, which allows using high number of antenn as without higher 4
transport bandwidth. Further, user data transfer can be optimized to send only PRBs that contain user data for 5
purpose of reducing transport bandwidth 6
- Beamforming Support: The same interface design can support different beamforming tech niques (digital, analog, 7
hybrid) as well as different beamforming algorithms. Likewise, deployments using only analog beamforming are 8
also possible with the same interface design. 9
- Interoperability: Less user specific parameters are used at split 7-2x (when compared to higher split options), 10
which can simplify specification. 11
- Advanced receivers and inter-cell coordination: this option allows implementation of advanced receivers and 12
coordination features, which are also easier to implement and less restricted when most functions are placed at 13
the O-DU.  For example, UL CoMP is not possible when the UL upper-PHY processing is in the O-RU. 14
- Lower O-RU complexity: Less functions at O-RU (when compared to higher split options) allow limiting the 15
number of required real time calculations as well as required memory requirement, especially for Category A O-16
RUs. 17
- Future proof-ness: Placing most functions at O-DU will allow introduction of new features via software upgrades 18
without inflicting HW changes at O-RU (e.g., specification changes due to URLLC or new modulation 19
schemes). 20
- Interface and functions symmetry: If the same interface and split point is used for DL and UL, specification effort 21
can be reduced. 22
 23

2.3 Latency Requirements 12
Intra-PHY lower layer fronthaul split has characteristic of a stringent bandwidth and tight latency requirement. This 13
implies use of a special “Fronthaul Service Profile” to be supported by the transport network, and which may differ 14
depending on the operating environment, topology and target use cases. The general concept and latency model is based 15
on eCPRI reference points for delay management definitions (See Figure 2-9). However, this specification provides 16
additional details for both DL and UL (Table 2-4) latency. The delay parameters and how these are determined are 17
explained below; note that in general it is expected a separate set of timing parameters applies to each O-RU attached to 18
an O-DU.  This chapter defines multiple approaches which can be used to provide an inter-operable timing solution. 19
The reference points defined for eCPRI are reflected below in Figure 2-9.  The reference points are: 20

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        23
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
• O-DU: R1/ R4 – Transmit/ Receive interface at O-DU 1
• O-RU: R2/ R3 – Receive/ Transmit interface at O-RU 2
• Ra: Antenna interface at O-RU 3
 4
 5
 6
Figure 2-9 : Definition of reference points for delay management (adapted from [3])  7
Transmission delay between O-DU and O-RU are specified as T12 (downlink) and T34 (uplink).  The transmission 8
delay encompasses only the time from when a bit leaves the sender (R1/ R3) until it is received at the receiver (R2/ R4).  9
In an ethernet transport network, these delays may not be constant due to switching delays (i.e. PDV).  To account for 10
this, transport delay must be considered as a range with upper and lower bounds:  11
• Downlink transport delay: T12min/ T12max 12
• Uplink transport delay: T34min/ T34max 13
However, fixed timing at Ra is still required.  Therefore, Ra is used as a reference point for delay management in the 14
eCPRI model.  Therefore, transmission and reception at the reference points are measured relative to Ra, resulting in the 15
following parameters: 16
Table 2-4 : eCPRI O-DU/ O-RU Delay Model Latency Parameters 17

Ta3min Ta3max
 18
It is expected that a common timescale is used on both ends of the link. The relative time error between the O-DU and 19
O-RU shall be within a limit of 3µs (±1.5 sec). This requirement applies whether the O-DU is on the synchronization 20
path towards O-RU or not (as specified in S-plane section 9). 21
2.3.1 Timing Parameter Relationships 22
To ensure proper reception of data at the receiver over the packet interface, there are several relationshi ps between the 23
parameters defined above which must be met.  First it is important to understand the nature of the transmission itself.  In 24
either direction (downlink/ uplink) it takes some amount of time for the sender to actually transmit the packets onto the 25
transmission media.  However, the amount of data for any interval (e.g symbol) can vary thus resulting differing 26
transmit times.  This transmission time can be affected by several factors including (but not limited to) transport media 27
rate, air interface bandwidth, and amount of data compression.   The maximum amount of time allowed for the 28
transmitter to send all data for an interval (Transmission Window) is defined by T1amax – T1amin.  It is important to 29
note that this is the allowed time, based on transport and O-RU characteristics.  This is explained in greater detail, and 30
its impacts on O-DU in section 2.3.1.1.     31
O-DU O-RU

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        24
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
To account for transport variation and transmission time the receiver implements a reception window.  This allows 1
packets containing samples for a specific symbol to be received within the window and still be  transmitted at Ra at the 2
required time.  The size of the Reception Window must account for both the maximum transmission time at the sender 3
and the transport variation through the fronthaul network.  The result is the first of the delay relationships whic h must 4
be met to ensure a working delay solution. 5
Reception Window ≥ Transmission Window + Transport Variation 6
Table 2-5 : eCPRI Delay Windows 7
 Reception Window Transmission Window Transport Variation
Downlink T2amax – T2amin T1amax – T1amin T12max – T12min
Uplink Ta4max – Ta4min Ta3max – Ta3min T34max – T34min
 8
The position (in time) of the reception/ transmission windows at the O-RU is fixed relative to the air interface.  9
However, the position of the corresponding windows at the O-DU is a function of the O-RU and transport parameters.  10
For guaranteed reception of packets sent from O-DU to O-RU within the O-RU reception window, the following 11
relationships must also be met1: 12
Table 2-6 : O-DU transmission/ reception window position (in time) 13
O-DU Timing Parameter O-DU Transmit Boundary Relationships
Downlink
(Transmit)
No earlier than T1amax T1amax ≤ T2amax + T12min
No later than T1amin T1amin ≥ T2amin + T12max
Uplink
(Receive)
No earlier than  Ta4min Ta4min ≤ Ta3min + T34min
No later than Ta4max Ta4max ≥ Ta3max + T34max

2.3.1.1 O-DU Transmission Window 15
The O-DU transmission window (T1amax – T1amin) is defined by the relationships above based on the O-RU reception 16
window and max transport variation.  It does not define the exact timing of transmission from the O-DU.  Rather, it 17
defines the boundaries that the O-DU transmission must operate within.  The window merely represents the 18
mathematical boundaries imposed on the O-DU as a result of the O-RU and Transport constraints.  It is possible to 19
define the constraints for any one of O-DU, transport and O-RU based on knowledge of the other two.  However, 20
typically the O-RU constraint is predefined based on the equipment and transport is part of the overall network goals.  21
The window resulting from the relationships must be greater than or equal to the actual maximum time required by the 22
O-DU to transmit all data for a symbol (TXmaxO-DU).  That is, the window must be at least large enough that the O-DU 23
can transmit in the worst case within the window.  Where, within the window, the O-DU transmits (e.g. beginning, 24
middle, end) and how much of the window is consumed by the O-DU transmission is a matter of O-DU design. 25
The following downlink example illustrates the concept: 26
• O-RU parameters:  T2amin = 100 usec, T2amax = 260 usec 27
• Transport Parameters (direct fiber of known length):  T12min = 50 usec, T12max = 51 usec 28
The result indicates an O-DU transmission window as follows: 29
• T1amax ≤ 260 + 50  30
• T1amin ≥ 100 + 51 31
This provides a very large transmit window available to the O-DU.  If, for example, the TXmaxO-DU is only 30 usec, 32
then the O-DU can determine where within the window to start its transmission, so long as the transmission completes 33
prior to T1amin. 34
If, however, this same O-DU were paired with an O-RU with smaller reception window (e.g. T2amin = 100, T2amax = 35
150) using a transport network with the same T12min, but with 15 usec of PDV (T12max = 65), the result is:  36
• T1amax ≤ 150 + 50 37
• T1amin ≥ 100 + 65 38

1 Refer to Annex B for explanation of these inequalities.

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        25
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
The delay solution still works (200 – 165 ≥ 30), but with far less margin, and far less flexibility as to where within the 1
window the O-DU may begin transmission. 2
2.3.2 U-Plane/ C-Plane Timing 3
The basic delay parameters above describe the general delay model and characteristics of the O-RAN interface.  4
However, the ORAN interface is divided into C-Plane and U-Plane parts.  The C-Plane must be available in order to 5
process the corresponding U-Plane packets.  To support coordination of C-Plane and U-Plane timing, the O-RAN 6
interface specifies that C-Plane messages must arrive at the O-RU some amount of time in advance (Tcp_adv_dl) of the 7
earliest possible time the corresponding U-Plane messages may arrive.  (Note that it is possible for Tcp_adv_dl to be set 8
to 0, but the O-RAN delay model does not assume this.)  As a result, O-RAN has defined the downlink timing 9
relationships and parameters shown in Figure 2-10. 10
 11
Figure 2-10 : Timing relations per symbol IQ in DL direction (U-Plane and C-Plane) 12
For simplicity O-RAN assumes that the transmission windows for C-Plane and U-Plane are the same size.  Tcp_adv_dl 13
allows for different alignment of the respective transmission/ reception windows as illustrated in the figure. The 14
reference (denoted as “tDL=0”) is the transmission of the earliest IQ sample in time domain within a symbol (including 15
cyclic prefix) which is generated from the IQ data received in a U-plane messages specific to a symbol identified by 16
symbolId (optionally adjusted by timeOffset).  17
A downlink C-plane message can refer to one or more symbols; transmission and reception windows for a downlink C -18
plane message referencing multiple symbols are relative to the start of the earliest symbol referenced by the message 19
(identified by startSymbolId; optionally adjusted by timeOffset). 20
For uplink, the C-Plane is sent from O-DU to O-RU, while the U-Plane is from O-RU to O-DU.  Since uplink C-Plane 21
is from O-DU to O-RU, it uses the R1/ R2 reference points, and corresponding timing parameters.  However, the uplink 22
C-Plane timing is not characterized relative to the downlink C-Plane or U-Plane timing, but relative to Ra.  The 23
resulting O-RAN delay model for non-PRACH channels is shown in Figure 2-11.  24

An uplink C-plane message not describing PRACH channels can refer to one or more symbols. The reference point for 3
uplink C-plane (denoted as “tUL=0”) is the reception of the earliest IQ sample (start of cyclic prefix) that is specific to 4
the earliest symbol referenced by the message (identified by startSymbolId; optionally adjusted by timeOffset). 5
The reference point for uplink U-plane messages related to non-PRACH channels (denoted as “tUL=0”) is the reception 6
of the earliest IQ sample in time domain within a symbol (including cyclic prefix) referenced by the message (identified 7
by symbolId; optionally adjusted by timeOffset). 8
Due to the characteristics regarding CP-length and symbol-length of PRACH-symbols, PRACH is handled in a separate 9
way. Especially for the long preamble PRACH formats 0..3 (see reference 3GPP TS 38.211) the length of both the CP 10
and the length of the repeatable part is much larger than corresponding parts for e.g. PUSCH symbols. For the other 11
PRACH formats (A1..C2) the length of the CP could be very long compared to the CP for the PUSCH symbols (up to 12
roughly 14 times longer (C2)). The consequence of either long CP and/or long repeatable part of specific PRACH 13
formats is that the values of Ta3_min and Ta3_max are increased, due to this, PRACH CP duration and number of 14
repetitive parts are excluded from the values for these parameters provided by O-RU. 15
The O-DU is aware of the characteristics of the PRACH format being used, e.g. length of CP, number of repetitions, 16
etc., with this information the position and size of the reception window is calculated by the O -DU according to Figure 17
2-12. The used values for Ta3_min and Ta3_max are the ones being retrieved from the O-RU for the SCS being used 18
for PRACH. Note that for calculating PRACH contribution to T2a_min_cp_ul, T2a_max_cp_ul, Ta3_min and Ta3_max 19
O-RU excludes PRACH CP (assume CP duration zero). 20

An uplink C-plane message for PRACH can refer to one or more repeatable parts of PRACH sequence (PRACH 1
repetitions). The reference point for PRACH C-plane message is time advanced by PRACH cyclic prefix duration from 2
the reception of the earliest IQ sample (excluding the PRACH cyclic prefix) in time domain that is specific to the 3
earliest PRACH repetition referenced by the message (identified by startSymbolId; optionally adjusted by 4
timeOffset). The reference point for PRACH U-plane is the reception of the earliest IQ sample (excluding cyclic prefix) 5
in time domain that is specific to the PRACH repetition (identified by symbol symbolId; optionally adjusted by 6
timeOffset and cpLength). 7
 8

for O-RU and O-DU define the delay profile parameters for the equipment. 10

 18
For the O-RAN interface to operate properly, the transmit and receive windows at the O-DU must be properly aligned.  19
(The O-RU window alignment is always based on Ra.)  For the O-DU to align transmit and receive windows, it needs 20
the following: 21
• O-RU Delay Characteristics 22
• Transport Network Delay Characteristics 23
The expected accuracy of the reported O-RU delay characteristics is 200ns.   The 200ns value was decided as a 24
compromise between a very high accuracy (tens of nanoseconds) which makes buffering in the O-RU easier and a more 25
modest accuracy (~0.5 microseconds) which is relatively easy for a well-controlled Ethernet network to accomplish.  26
This accuracy applies only to the reported start and end times of the reception and transmission windows relative to Ra.  27
Ra is a fixed reference point as defined by the respective air interface standards and supported features.  As an example, 28
if the O-RU reports T2a_min_up of 20 µs, the O-RU may begin processing the data from 19.8 µs to 20.2 µs prior to the 29

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        28
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
symbol over the air transmission time (Ra).  However, the O-RU MUST still transmit over the air with the precision as 1
defined by the air interface standards. 2
The remainder of this chapter addresses O-RAN supported methods for providing the O-DU with the required 3
information to properly align transmit and receive windows.  The following methods are supported:  4

2.3.3 Computed Latency Methods 7
As previously noted, the goal of delay management for O-RAN interfaces is to ensure that the transmission/ reception 8
windows as the O-DU are properly aligned to support the O-RU and transport network characteristics.  The computed 9
methods require the O-DU to compute the required transmit and receive windows based on O-RU delay and transport 10
network characteristics. The O-DU then determines if the O-DU can accommodate the required transmit and receive 11
windows.  If so, it sets its transmit and receive windows (based on O-DU design) to the most appropriate settings.  As 12
an example, an O-DU may have a fixed set of transmit and receive windows.  The O-DU may then select the fixed 13
settings that best fit the transport latency (T12/ T34) and O-RU delay characteristics for the connected O-RUs. 14
The O-DU should raise some notification if it is not capable of accommodating the required transmit and receive 15
windows.   16
Alignment of the transmit window at the O-DU requires that the following conditions be met based on the downlink 17
delay relationships defined in Table 2-8: 18
• The O-DU MUST be able to transmit DL U-Plane (T1a_min_up/ T1a_max_up) 19
• Early enough (e.g. before T1a_min_up) to ensure it is received at O-RU before T2a_min_up 20
• Late enough (e.g. after T1a_max_up) to ensure it is NOT received before T2a_max_up 21
• Similarly, the O-DU must be able to transmit C-Plane messages within their respective windows 22
Alignment of the receive window at the O-DU requires that the following conditions be met based on the uplink delay 23
relationships defined in Table 2-9 : 24
• The O-DU MUST set its receive window for UL U-Plane (Ta4_min, Ta4_max) 25
• Early enough to ensure UL U-Plane is not received before Ta4_min 26
• Late enough to ensure UL U-Plane is not received after Ta4_max 27
The following O-DU constraints must be met for delay management to operate properly: 28
 29
 30

If any of the O-DU constraints is violated, then proper delay operation cannot be assured.   1
2.3.3.1 Fronthaul Timing Domain 2
When using a computed delay approach, the domain over which the O-DU delay parameters apply must be considered.  3
A timing domain is defined as the set of O-DU and O-RU ports to which the computed O-DU delay parameters apply.  4
The O-DU timing domain may encompass a single O-DU port, or may encompass multiple O-DU ports, where the O-5
DU requires the same O-DU timing parameters to apply to all O-DU ports in the domain. 6
Similarly, each O-DU port in the domain may support O-RAN C/U-Plane traffic to 1 or more O-RU ports.  Therefore, a 7
fronthaul timing domain encompasses one or more DU_Port_IDs and one or more RU_port_IDs. 8
When computing the O-DU transmit/ receive windows, the single set of delay parameters for the O-DU must 9
encompass the worst case of Transport and O-RU delay characteristics within the domain.  The following definitions 10
are used: 11
• Transport Network Parameters for timing domain 12
 T12_minij : T12_min between DU_Port_ID(i) and RU_Port_ID(j) 13
T34_minij : T34_min between DU_Port_ID(i) and RU_Port_ID(j) 14
Note: it is assumed that if a timing domain has multiple T12_min/ T34_min values, then the transport delay 15
characteristics are measured.  For measured transport delay it is not possible to measure the maximum delay.  T12_max 16
and T34_max in this case is computed by adding an a pre-defined (e.g. via SLA) worst case variation (PDVmax) to the 17
corresponding transport minimum delay values. 18
Table 2-12: Radio Delay Parameters for O-RU Port "j" within timing domain 19
 Downlink U-Plane Uplink U-Plane Downlink C-Plane2 Uplink C-Plane
Minimum T2a_min_upj Ta3_minj T2a_min_cp_dlj T2a_min_cp_ulj
Maximum T2a_max_upj Ta3_maxj T2a_max_cp_dlj T2a_max_cp_ulj
  20
The resulting parameters to be used for determining the O-DU window for the timing domain are: 21
Table 2-13 : Transport Network Parameters for Timing Domain 22
 Downlink Uplink
Minimum T12_min = MIN(T12_minij) T34_min = MIN(T34_minij)
Maximum T12_max = MAX(T12_minij) + PDVmax T34_max = MAX(T34_minij) + PDVmax
 23
Table 2-14 : O-RU Delay Parameters for Timing Domain 24
  U-Plane C-Plane
Downlink Minimum T2a_min_up = MAX(T2a_min_upj) T2a_min_cp_dl = MAX(T2a_min_cp_dlj)
Maximum T2a_max_up = MIN(T2a_max_upj) T2a_max_cp_dl = MIN(T2a_max_cp_dlj)
Uplink Minimum Ta3_min = MIN(Ta3_minj) T2a_min_cp_ul = MAX(T2a_min_cp_ulj)
Maximum Ta3_max = MAX(Ta3_maxj) T2a_max_cp_ul = MIN(T2a_max_cp_ulj)
The tables defined above are used to derive the corresponding delay parameters to be used in the timing relationships 25
defined in Table 2-8 and Table 2-9  to support the computed methods defined below. 26
When using a computed delay approach, there are two situations that are considered within O-RAN: 27
• Defined Transport Method: the network delay is pre-defined (usually by the network operator) and the 28
delays are computed based on that definition.  The definition generally considers the maximum network 29
latency, with an assumption that a smaller delay can more easily be accommodated. 30
• Measured Transport method: the network delay is estimated based on actual measurements of packet latency 31
on the downlink and uplink (sometimes a symmetric delay is assumed but this is not necessary).  32
 33
Regardless of whether the Defined Transport Method or Measured Transport Method is used, the calcu lations are 34
mainly the same.  The next two sub-sections describe in more detail these two methods. 35

2 Tcp_adv_dl may be different across RUs within a domain, therefore T2a_min_cp_dl and T2a_max_cp_dl must be used to determine
downlink C-Plane window.

2.3.3.2 Defined Transport Method 2
With the Defined Transport method, the O-DU transmit and receive windows are determined based on pre -defined 3
transport network characteristics, and the delay characteristics of the O-RUs within the timing domain.  For this 4
approach to work, the O-RU delay characteristics as defined in Table 2-14 for each O-RU in the timing domain must be 5
available to the O-DU.  The O-DU can adapt its transmit and receive windows to accommodate the O-RU delay 6
characteristics if O-DU has that capability.   The adaptation of the O-DU transmission and reception windows may be 7
limited based on the O-DU design. 8
Table 2-15 : Delay Profiles 9
 O-RU delay profile O-DU delay profile
Downlink
T2a_min_up, T2a_max_up,

Uplink Ta3_min, Ta3_max, T2a_min_cp_ul,

Ta4_maxO-DU, RXmaxO-DU
 10
Refer to M-Plane specification for details on how O-RU parameters are obtained.  Optionally, an O-RU may adapt its 11
delay profile information, especially for uplink, based on O-DU delay profile and transport delay (T12_min/ T34_min).  12
If this option is supported, then the O-DU must provide its delay profile as well as the T12_min to the O-RU.  The 13
exchange of this information should use the same approach as defined for exchange of O-RU delay profile.  With this 14
option, the O-RU receives the O-DU delay profile before sending its delay profile to the O-DU.  The O-DU is unaware 15
that the O-RU delay profile information has been modified.   It simply uses the delay profile provided by the O-RU as it 16
would without this option. 17
As previously noted, delay characteristics for an O-RU may vary based on air interface properties.  To ensure 18
interoperability, O-RAN supported air interface properties which may be used as the basis for supporting different delay 19
characteristics are limited to: 20
- Channel Bandwidth 21
- SCS 22
 23
A set of delay characteristics which applies to a combination of the above properties is referred to as a delay profile.  24
For each supported combination of the above properties that an O-RU supports, a delay profile must be identified.  Note 25
it is possible for multiple combinations of the above properties to utilize the same O-RU delay profile.  These delay 26
profiles are O-RU specific, and are not within the scope of the O-RAN specification.   27
When calculating the O-DU transmit and receive window for a timing domain, the O-DU must use the delay profile 28
applicable for each O-RU based on the air interface properties used by the O-RU in the specific network configuration.  29
As previously noted, it is possible that the O-DU design is not capable of adjusting its transmit/ delay windows to meet 30
the required transmit or receive windows.   31
The O-RU and O-DU may have multiple delay profiles depending on the design. The following Table 2-16 indicates 32
the contents of each profile.   33

2.3.3.3 Measured Transport Method 3
With the Measured Transport method, the O-DU transmit and receive windows are determined based on the delay 4
characteristics of the O-RUs as defined in section 2.3.3.2, and measured transport delays between all O-DU ports and 5
O-RU ports in the timing domain.  The O-DU must measure the transport delay(s) for all O-RU/O-DU port pairs in the 6
timing domain and adapt its transmit and receive windows to accommodate the measured transport delay 7
characteristics. 8
O-RAN specifies the use of the One-Way Delay Measurement messages as defined in the eCPRI specification.  While 9
other methods are possible, both endpoints must support a common approach.  Some of the benefits of using the eCPRI 10
approach are: 11
• Same ethernet type and address (and IP address/ UDP port) between O-DU and O-RU as used for C-Plane and 12
U-Plane in order to obtain consistent measurements. 13
• eCPRI One-Way Delay Measurement allows for varying packet sizes to better simulate real traffic.  14
• eCPRI approach allows for separate T12 and T34 measurements, and measurements in both directions may be 15
initiated by either communication peer. 16
Note that for this delay measurement eCPRI must be used because no other specified transport mechanism supports 17
one-way delay measurements – this is true even if RoE is used as the C-Plane and U-Plane transport mechanism. 18
 19
Table 2-17 : eCPRI One-Way Delay Measurement Message 20
One-Way Delay Measurement (Type 5)
0  (msb) 1 2 3 4 5 6 7  (lsb) # of
bytes

ecpriVersion ecpriReserved ecpriConcat
enation
1 Octet 1
ecpriMessage = 5 1 Octet 2
ecprPayload 2 Octet 3
Measurement ID 1 Octet 5

TimeStamp (seconds) 6 Octet 7
TimeStamp (nanoseconds) 4 Octet 13
Compensation value (nanoseconds) 8 Octet 17

                  Octet M
 21
The O-DU initiates the measurements for T12 by sending a One-Way Delay Measurement request to the O-RU.  The O-22
RU responds to the One-Way Delay Measurement response message.  The request has a timestamp based on the 23
transmission time at the O-DU (t1) and a Compensation value to account for expected processing time from t1 until 24
actual packet transmission at R1. 25
The O-RU timestamps the received request message (t2).  The O-RU creates a response message which contains the 26
receive timestamp (t2) and a compensation value to account for expected delay from packet receipt at R2 to 27
timestamping in the O-RU.  O-DU then computes T12 using the formula indicated in Figure 2-13.  tcv1 and tcv2 are 28
implementation-specific “compensation” values an O-DU and O-RU may use to accommodate known timestamp 29
processing delays.  Note that this measurement process including the definition of the terms in the figure is described in 30
the eCPRI specification [3].   31

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        32
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
 1
Figure 2-13 : eCPRI One-Way Delay Measurement procedure (Figure 25 of [3]) 2
Note that when measuring the one-way delay, the goal is to determine the minimum transport delay.  Since any 3
individual packet can experience different delays through the network due to PDV, it is necessary to perform multiple 4
measurements.  Since the desired value is the minimum (T12_min or T34_min), the minimum delay measurement 5
among the various measurements is used to estimate the minimum transport delay.  The O-DU may use the estimated 6
T12_min value as the T34_min value.  It is optional for the O-DU to request the O-RU to perform a measurement of 7
T34 using the One-Way Delay Measurement remote request.   8
There is no theoretical upper bound for transport delay.  The maximum values are determined based on the measured 9
minimum values plus a maximum allowed transport variation (PDVmax) defined as part of the SLA for the transport 10
network. 11
The O-DU must measure the delay corresponding to each O-DU/ O-RU port pair within the timing domain.  The per 12
port pair minimum delay are then used as per Table 2-12 to provide the transport parameters to be used in the delay 13
relationships specified in Table 2-8 and Table 2-9 .  The resulting delay configuration must meet the O-DU constraints 14
defined in Table 2-11. 15
Since the transport delay is necessary to establish correct timing, this measurement is performed initially before sending 16
of C/U-Plane traffic.  The measurement should be performed with packets using the same L2 Cos Priority or L3 DSCP 17
Code as the U-Plane data uses, to ensure the measurement accurately reflects the U-Plane transport latency. This could 18
also be done periodically to verify delay if desired.  Note however that section 2.4 d escribes traffic counts which can 19
also be used for error detection.  If an error is detected, it may be necessary to re-measure the delay and update the O-20
DU timing based on the new measurements. 21
2.3.4 Latency Categories for O-DU with dynamic timing advance  22
As an aide to selection of equipment for use in specific transport network use cases, O-RAN O-DU and O-RU are 23
categorized based on delay capabilities.  Equipment is categorized with a Category [A AAA-ZZ] and Sub-category [.00-24

Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        33
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
.1000].  The category can be used to determine the maximum T12max/ T34max which the equipment can support.  1
Categories are determined as follows: 2
• T1a_maxO-DU ≥ T12max + T2a_min_up 3
• Ta4_maxO-DU ≥ T34max + Ta3_max_up 4
 5
By definition T1a_max_upO-DU  is the earliest that the O-DU can begin transmission.  This results in that the earliest 6
that the O-DU can be guaranteed to complete transmission is T1a_max_upO-DU - TXmaxO-DU.  The result is that: 7
• T1a_max_upO-DU - TXmaxO-DU - T2a_min_up ≥ T12max  8
Similarly, for uplink Ta4_max_upO-DU is the latest that the O-DU may receive any packet from the O-RU.  Therefore: 9
• Ta4_max_upO-DU - Ta3_max_up ≥ T34max  10

Note that the maximum range that can be supported is based on the minimum value of T12_max/ T34_max from above.  18
This requires separate categorization for UL and DL for each O-DU and O-RU.  Therefore, an uplink category pair 19
(AX) and downlink category pair (BY) must be evaluated for each pairing of O-DU and O-RU.  The minimum distance 20
(T12_max/ T34_max) represented by the corresponding pairs (AX/ BY) then identifies the maximum transport latency 21
which can be supported by the pair. 22
Each endpoint has a specific value for the characteristics defined in the table immediately above.  In order to categorize 23
endpoints, the endpoints are grouped based on the range in which the maximum transport characteristic falls.  The 24
category identification then means that the maximum transport characteristic for the endpoint falls somewhere between 25
the maximum and minimum values for the category.   26
Since the category identifies that the maximum latency supported for the endpoint falls somewhere within the category 27
range, the resulting category pair (AX) defines a range of T12_max/ T34_max for the combination.  The combination 28
can be used to identify a maximum T12_max/ T34_max and minimum T12_max/ T34_max that the combination can 29
support.  The category pair identifies a best and worst case for the maximum range the combination can support.  The 30
actual maximum range supported by a specific O-DU/ O-RU with a given category combination will fall somewhere 31
within the range.  That is, for a given O-DU with category A and O-RU with category X, the T12_max/ T34_max 32
supported by the pair is defined as: 33
• Latency_minAX ≤ (T12_max = T34_max) ≤ Latency_maxAX 34

Additionally, the paired combination can be mapped to supported use cases.  Annex B. 1 contains the category 1
definitions, Latency_min, Latency_max, and use case tables. 2
Note that categories are only useful for identifying the maximum possible range (T12_max/ T34_max) values.  To be 3
able to usefully identify equipment for a given use-case, it is also necessary to be able to identify T12_min/ T34_min 4
values.  This can be defined in terms of variation, where variation indicates how much lower T12_min/ T34_min can be 5
relative to T12_max/ T34_max respectively.  That is: 6
• DL Variation = T12_max – T12_min 7
• UL Variation = T34_max – T34_min 8
An additional level of categorization is required to address the transport delay variation (e.g. T12max – T12min) that a 9
given equipment type pair can support.  This is addressed using sub-categories.  Note that the total variation that can be 10
supported is a combination of the receive window at the receiver, and the transmit variation.  However, the receive 11
window will likely be much larger than the transmit variation.  Each endpoint is assigned to both an uplink and 12
downlink category.  The O-DU DL sub-category is based on transmit variation, while the O-DU UL sub-category is 13
based on receive window.  For UL, the roles are reversed and the O-RU DL sub-category is based on receive window, 14
and the UL sub-category is based on transmit variation.   15
As a result, sub-categories are defined based on the role for the endpoint in the given transmission direction (UL/ DL).  16
Downlink variation (Tvarition_DL) is defined in Figure 2-15 as follows: 17

Sub-categories for each direction are defined using the endpoint characteristi cs.  Similar to categories, sub-categories 3
indicate that the endpoint has a variation characteristic that falls somewhere in the specified sub -category range.  Since 4
transmit variability and receive windows are expected to have different ranges, separate table s are created.  However, 5
since sub-categories are numeric [.####], to simplify these values are assigned in a similar way.  The #### value 6
represents the time in 10’s of µsec for the given endpoint property. 7
For example, if an O-RU with a category X has a T2a_min – T2a_max = 163 µsec, the sub-category for the O-RU 8
would be [.16].  Making the full category for the O-RU X.16 (the “.” is not a decimal point but a delimiter only).  Note 9
that by using this approach, the receiver sub-category and transmitter sub-category can be directly used to determine the 10
dynamic range in either direction: 11
TX sub-category: .06 12
RX sub-category: .16 13
Resulting Tvariability = 16 * 10 - 6 * 10 = 100 µsec.  Note that this provides a lower bound on the maximum dynamic 14
range supported in one direction.  The minimum of the UL and DL Tvariability is used to determine the maximum dynamic 15
range for the combination (e.g. maximum difference between T12_max and T12_min that can be supported) . 16
Annex B.2 contains the sub-category definitions and resulting combination dynamic range use case tables. 17
With dynamic timing advance, since the O-DU can set its earliest transmission time based on the measured T12_max, 18
the full range of variability is available regardless of the T12_max.  For example, if an O-DU/ O-RU combination has 19
100 usec worth of variability, and a maximum range T12_max of 250 usec, the combination can support the measured 20
T12_max (≤ 250 usec) and T12_min of T12_max – 100 usec.  So, if the measured T12_max is 200 usec, the 21
combination can support T12_max of 200 usec and T12_min of 100 usec. 22
2.3.5 Latency Categories for O-DU with fixed timing advance  23
The same category concepts apply for O-DU which support fixed transmit/ receive windows.  The earliest transmit 24
window and latest receive window timing defines the maximum range that the O-DU can support.  T1a_max_upO-DU   25
for the O-DU is equal to the T1a_max_up of the earliest fixed transmit window.  Similarly, Ta4_maxO-DU  is equal to the 26
Ta4_max of the latest receive window.  The O-DU category is then assigned based on this value.   27
Fixed transmit and receive windows do however impact variability.  The fixed transmit time results in sending pac kets 28
earlier than absolutely needed to achieve the actual T12_max (i.e. the T12_max value that is either measured or 29
configured by the service provider).  The following figure Figure 2-17 illustrates: 30
 31

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        36
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
 1
Figure 2-17 : Fixed Transmit Time Illustration 2
Note that since the TXDL_Start is earlier than required for T12_max by T1a_offset, the latest packets arrive at least 3
T1a_offset before T2a_min_up.  This results in T1a_offset worth of O-RU receive buffer being always used, implying 4
that the O-RU needs additional buffer to compensate T1a_offset.  Since the TXDL_Start is fixed, the T12min is also fixed 5
at: 6
T12min = TXDL_Start  – (T2a_max_up – TXmaxO-DU) 7
Since T12min is unaffected, the resulting Tvariation is reduced by T1a_offset.  To support T12min, O-RU need to provide 8
T1a_offset + T12max – T12min + TXmax_O_DU  9
It should also be noted that when T12_max + T2a_min_up = T1a_max_upO-DU (e.g. at maximum range), then 10
T1a_offset = 0.  But as the actual T12_max decreases, the amount of receive buffer consumed by T1a_offset increases 11
by the corresponding amount. 12
Note that the same applies for UL.  The result is that the T34_min is defined relative to the T34_max for the 13
combination, not relative to the measured T34_max.   14
2.3.6 Non-Delay Managed U-Plane Traffic 15
While most U-Plane data must meet the delay constraints described above, there are certain types of U -Plane traffic for 16
which the reception windows may not be applicable.  Not applying the transmission/ reception window constraints to 17
such data allows the transmission of the data over the fronthaul interface to be spread to reduce the peak bandwidth 18
required by the interface.   19
Figure 2-18 uses SRS to illustrate an example non-delay managed U-Plane traffic. 20
O-DU
O-DU
O-DU

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        37
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
 1
Figure 2-18 : SRS as non-delay managed U-Plane traffic 2
In the example, a large amount of SRS data is received during a special slot on the air interface.  By allowing the SRS 3
data to be transmitted from the O-RU to the O-DU outside of the normal transmit window, and instead allowing it to 4
use otherwise un-used fronthaul interface bandwidth, the required peak fronthaul bandwidth requirement can be 5
substantially reduced.   6
Non-delay managed U-Plane traffic flows must be uniquely identified from delay managed U-Plane traffic flows.  The 7
identification of U-Plane traffic flows using eAxC identifiers is described in section 3.4, and the eAxC must be used to 8
indicate delay-managed versus non-delay-managed traffic (e.g. some eAxC or Pcid-identified packets may carry delay-9
managed U-Plane traffic while other eAxC or Pcid-identified packets may carry non-delay-managed U-Plane traffic).  10
Non-delay managed U-Plane traffic flows may not use the same eAxC identifiers as delay managed U -Plane traffic 11
flows.  Different RU_Port_IDs are allocated within the c_eAxC ID for non -delay managed U-Plane flows from those 12
used for delay managed U-Plane traffic flows in the same direction (UL/DL).  It is not required that the RU_Port_IDs 13
used in UL for non-delay managed traffic be the same as those used in the DL.  14
Non-delay managed traffic may be sent by the sender without regard to the transmission window for the corresponding 15
air interface symbol.  Similarly, the receiver will not discard received non-delay managed traffic received outside the 16
normal reception window for the corresponding air interface symbol.  However, the air interface symbol time is still 17
contained within the U-Plane packets for reference.   18
Note that the status of traffic flows being delay-managed versus non-delay-managed (which is managed within the 19
application) is independent of any transport prioritization that may be configured.  20
 2.4  Reception Window Monitoring 21
The reception window can be used for the purpose of error detection. The reception windows at O-RU and O-DU 22
absorb delay variation in the fronthaul. Although its maximum size may vary depending on implementation, the 23
position of windows at O-DU may be adjustable via parametrization as described in section 2.3.  24
Data flow error detection during fronthaul operation is monitored and reported via counters based on receipt of packets 25
relative to the reception window. The receiving node monitors the actual reception timing and generates counters 26
regarding the status and whether packets were successfully received in proper timing.  27
The following reception window performance counters are expected to be supported: 28
 29
• Data was received on time (within reception window) – no action needed 30
• Data was received too early – link latency re-measurement may be needed 31

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        38
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
• Data was received too late – link latency re-measurement may be needed 1
The above counters and other performance monitoring counters may be implemented as defined in section 7.1.  Based 2
on those counts the system may adjust its operation.   The specific reaction will be implementation-specific and depend 3
on such considerations as the number of instances needed before justifying sending an alarm, the ability of the system to 4
measure link latencies, or other design-dependent judgments.  Additional details regarding the counts, alarms, and fault 5
handling is outside of the scope of this current document. 6
Dynamic adaptation of windows at Tx side to handle larger delay values may be employed.  For instance, fronthaul 7
distance may be extended by handling a portion (or most) of the fixed delay component at Tx buffer side by adapting 8
the transmission timing based on information provided by Rx side. In other words, rather than accommodating a latency 9
range of zero to “max”, it is possible to instead accommodate a longer latency of “offset” to “max+offset” (longer 10
latency but same range).  This is described in detail in section 2.3. 11

Chapter 3 Transport & Protocol Architecture 1
3.1 Transport Encapsulation Types 2
3.1.1 Ethernet Encapsulation 3
Ethernet can be used as transport mechanism for both U-plane and C-plane. In this case, messages are transmitted over 4
standard Ethernet frames (See Figure 3-1). The supported frame format is Ethernet II/DIX frame with type 5
interpretation of the type length field. The Length-interpretation and multiplexing data with LLC (IEEE 802.2 LLC 6
standard) is not supported. A globally administered unicast MAC address is assigned per Ethernet interface.  The 7
Ethernet termination feature supports MAC Client Data field sizes of up to 1500 bytes for basic frames, and up to 9000 8
bytes for jumbo frames. 9
To ensure QoS is supported, 3-bit PCP field as defined in IEEE 802.1Q shall be supported meaning the capability for 10
VLAN tagging for the traffic shall be supported and M-Plane configurable (but using VLAN tags may be omitted for 11
certain network configurations e.g. LLS-C1). Priority tagged frames (i.e., frames tagged with VLAN ID=0) are always 12
received, as required by standard. Transmitting priority tagged frames is optional.  13
The MAC address resolution for U-plane and C-plane is performed via an M-plane procedure specified in [11] 14
regardless of whether the destined node supports IP or not. 15
The O-DU and O-RU shall support Intermediate L2 switch MAC address learning to avoid flooding U-plane and/or C-16
plane (not necessary for topology LLS-C1); this is done as a result of test Ethernet frame exchanges during the M-Plane 17
procedure “Ethernet connectivity checking” as specified in [11]. 18
Further, both the eCPRI header and payload are contained within the Ethernet data field.  For this encapsulation, either 19
the eCPRI Ethertype or the IEEE 1914.3 Ethertype shall be used. 20
 21
 22
Figure 3-1 : Native Ethernet frame with VLAN 23
 24
3.1.2 IP/UDP Encapsulation 25
IP/UDP can be used as transport mechanism for both U-plane and C-plane. In this case, IP version 4 shall be supported 26
according to RFC791 (Internet Protocol), and/or IPv6 as per RFC2460 (both IPv4 and IPv6 are optional, see Table 8-2).  27
The encapsulation mechanism is identified by “IPv4” or “IPv6” Ethertype (See Figure 3-2 and Figure 3-3).  O-RAN 28
equipment shall not send IPv4 packets with any IPv4 header option field.  If the IPv4 header option field is detected in 29
the received packet, the packet is to be silently discarded at the receiving equipment (though the existence of the 30
discarded packet may be logged). 31
IP based applications can be bound to virtual or physical interface addresses. Each configured physical and logical (i.e. 32
VLAN-) interface shall be configured with a separate subnet. As an option, Alias IP addresses (i.e. more than one IP 33
address on the same interface) can be supported as well. If supported, they can be assigned to logical and physical 34
interfaces as well as to virtual interfaces (loopback interfaces). 35
ORAN packets shall not use IPv4 or IPv6 fragmentation due to stringent bandwidth and tight latency requirements 36
(such received packets shall be silently discarded). The minimum MTU that is configurable shall be as per the relevant 37
standard, i.e. RFC791 for IPv4 and RFC2460 for IPv6. 38
The resolution of MAC addresses and the flooding traffic reduction through L2 switch mac address le arning shall be 39
done via ARP according to RFC 826 only when IPv4 is supported by the transmitting and receiving nodes.  40
When using IP/UDP encapsulation, the UDP destination port field shall identify the encapsulated protocol.  The UDP 41
destination port field shall be set during initialization via M-Plane configuration.  When the eCPRI Specification or 42
IEEE1914.3 define their respective UDP destination ports these will become the default for M-Plane configuration.. 43

3.1.3 Transport Headers 15
O-RAN allows for multiple different transport headers within the Ethernet payload to further describe how the application 16
data is to be handled in the C-Plane and U-Plane.  In each case the transport header is 8 bytes in length and provides basic 17
data routing capabilities, including description of the data fl ow type, sending and reception port identifiers, ability to 18
support concatenation of multiple application messages in a single Ethernet packet, and sequence numbering.  The 19
following sections describe the possible transport headers. 20

3.1.3.1.6 ecpriRtcid / ecpriPcid (real time control data / IQ data transfer message series 5
identifier) 6
Description: This parameter is a component_eAxC identifier (c_eAxC ID) and identifies the specific data flow 7
associated with each C-Plane (ecpriRtcid) or U-Plane (ecpriPcid) message.  It is the analog of CPRI’s “AxC” (antenna-8
carrier) value so is designated here as “eAxC” (“e” for “extended” to accommodate multiple bands and multiple 9
component carriers).  In addition, the “eAxC” is divided into “component eAxC” parts (c_eAxC) because multiple O-10
DU processors may contribute to a single eAxC and must be identified for correct data routing.  11
 An endpoint may support mixed numerologies by indicating the capability via an M-plane parameter; and a Section 12
Type 3 message can be used to select one of the numerologies (frameStructure) from the capability list of possible 13
numerologies for that endpoint.  In this case, a single c_eAxC id may be used for channels with different characteristics 14
(framestructure, cpLength, timeOffset, freqOffset), e.g. channels with different numerology and PRACH can share 15
same c_eAxC Id.  Alternatively, an endpoint may be simpler, supporting a single numerology by declaring non-support 16
of mixed numerology capability via the M-plane parameter, and a Section Type 3 message can be used to convey 17
different values for parameters other than frameStructure than the M-plane configured value.  In this case a unique 18
c_eAxC id would be used for each mixed numerology channel (frameStructure), i.e. channels with different 19
numerologies will use different c_eAxC ids.  An endpoint may declare non-support for Section Type 3, in which case it 20
is suitable for channels like PDSCH/PUSCH. 21
The c_eAxC ID value assigned to an O-RU’s endpoint is used to identify the endpoint within O-RU and must be unique 22
among all endpoints of O-RU of the same direction (Tx or Rx). An O-RU’s endpoint could in principle be associated 23
with multiple physical and virtual fronthaul interfaces (Ethernet ports and VLANs), but a c_eAxC ID value must be 24
unique and cannot be used to address different endpoints. The association of an O -RU’s endpoint with multiple 25
fronthaul interfaces is subject to O-RU design restrictions reported by O-RU via the M-Plane – interfaces not associated 26
with a given endpoint shall not be configured via M-plane for transferring C- and U-plane message to the endpoint. 27
Also, fronthaul interfaces not configured via M-plane shall not be used for transferring C- and U-plane messages to the 28
endpoint.  In other words, the O-DU shall obey the O-RU restrictions as conveyed on the M-Plane. 29
The O-DU may decide to use same c_eAxC ID value to address endpoints on different O -RUs, but c_eAxC ID values 30
assigned to one O-RU must be unique within the O-RU’s endpoints of same direction. 31
It is noted that there are O-RUs which do not support, within a same eAxC ID, independent sequence checkers for C-32
plane messages describing U-plane DL and C-plane messages describing U-plane UL, which is specified in Section 33
3.1.3.1.7. This O-RU capability limitation can be signalled by O-RUs and be interpreted by O-DUs from M-plane 34
specification v03.00. If either or both of the O-RU and O-DU only supports previous versions of the M-plane 35
specification, then the network operators and vendors intending to work with such O-RUs will need to ensure that the 36
O-DU can interpret the O-RU capability limitation in other non-standardized ways, i.e. through off-line discussions. To 37
interoperate with such O-RUs, O-DUs will need to avoid requiring at the O-RU, within a same eAxC ID, independent 38
sequence checkers for C-plane messages describing U-plane DL and C-plane messages describing U-plane UL, either 39
by (1) using different eAxC IDs for them; or (2) if the O-DU chooses to use the same eAxC ID, using a shared sequence 40
generator for C-plane messages describing U-plane DL and C-plane messages describing U-plane UL as shown in 41
Figure 3-6 and described in Section 3.1.3.1.7. However, support of such behavior in order to interoperate with such O-42

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        43
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
RUs is optional for the O-DUs, and it is noted that incompatible operation is likely to result in data outages and spurious 1
error reports. 2
This version of specification does not define behavior regarding transferring C - and U-plane messages to or from an 3
endpoint over multiple fronthaul interfaces at the same time.  For example, in UL, if an endpoint were to be assigned to 4
multiple fronthaul ports, there is no way to instruct the endpoint which fronthaul port to use for any given message . In 5
addition, in DL there is no accommodation for multiple delay windows per c_eAxC ID when multiple links are used.  6
Therefore, an endpoint shall be assigned exactly one fronthaul interface via the M-Plane, but different endpoints may 7
use different fronthaul interfaces (ports) so the O-RU as a whole may use multiple fronthaul ports. 8
eAxC ID subfields 9
One eAxC identifier (eAxC ID) comprises a band and sector identifier (BandSector_ID), a component -carrier identifier 10
(CC_ID) and a spatial stream identifier (RU_Port_ID).  One eAxC identi fier can consist of one or several c_eAxC IDs 11
having same BandSector_ID, CC-ID, and RU_Port_ID, but different values of DU_Port_ID, the different values 12
correlating with different O-DU processors.   13
Note: This parameter is specific to eCPRI Message Type = 2 (C-Plane) and Message Type 0 (U-Plane) messages. In 14
this version of the specification, one eAxC contains only one spatial stream (i.e. one beam per subcarrier) at a time.   15
When precoding in the O-RU, then each eAxC contains one layer at a time, except for TxD (LTE TM2) when a single 16
eAxC (single ecpriRtcid and ecpriPcid) represents all TxD layers. 17
Bit allocation is subdivided as follows: 18
o DU_Port_ID: Used to differentiate processing units at O-DU (e.g., different baseband cards).  It is expected the 19
O-DU will assign these bits, and the O-RU will attach the same value to the UL U-Plane messages carrying the 20
same sectionId data. 21
o BandSector_ID: Aggregated cell identifier (distinguishes bands and sectors supported by the O-RU). 22
o CC_ID: distinguishes Carrier Components supported by the O-RU. 23
o RU_Port_ID: designates logical flows such as data layers or spatial streams, and logical flows such as separate 24
numerologies (e.g. PRACH) or signaling channels requiring special antenna assignments such as SRS.   The 25
assignment of RU_Port_ID as part of the eAxC is done by the O-DU via the M-plane. 26
The bitwidth of each of the above fields is variable and set via M-Plane messaging.   This is to allow flexibility given it 27
is expected that not all fields will simultaneously need their maximum range for any given O-RU.  It is expected the M-28
Plane message will configure the O-RU and O-DU with the appropriate bitwidth of each of the four fields, and the NMS 29
that does the actual assignment will assure all 16 bits are allocated (with or without padding).  30
Value range: {0000 0000 0000 0000b-1111 1111 1111 1111b = eAxC ID} 31

3.1.3.1.7 ecpriSeqid (message identifier) 37
Description: This parameter provides unique message identification and ordering on two different levels. The first octet 38
of this parameter is the Sequence ID, which is used to identify ordering of messages within an eAxC message stream.  39
The Sequence ID field increments and wraps independently for each U-Plane eAxC DL, U-Plane eAxC UL, C-Plane 40
eAxC DL, and C-Plane eAxC UL, even if they share the same eAxC ID. The Sequence ID is used to verify that all 41
messages are received and also to reorder messages that are received out of order. The second octet of this parameter is 42
the Subsequence ID. The Subsequence ID is used to verify ordering and implement reordering when ra dio-transport-43
level (eCPRI or IEEE-1914.3) fragmentation occurs.  Radio-transport (eCPRI or IEEE-1914.3) fragmentation is a 44
method of splitting U-plane messages containing one or more sections whose length exceeds the maximum packet or 45
message length of the underlying protocol.  The Subsequence ID field consists of a 7 bit Subsequence counter and a 46

________________________________________________________________________________________________
Copyright © 2020 by the O-RAN Alliance.
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ        44
O-RAN.WG4.CUS.0-v03.00
single bit field, called E-bit. The Subsequence number increments starting from zero for each fragment of a U-plane 1
message.  The E bit is used to indicate the last message of the radio-transport level fragments.  It is always set to zero 2
except for the last message of the U-plane fragment. In the case of C-plane messages radio-transport fragmentation is 3
not allowed, therefore the Subsequence ID shall be set to zero, and the E bit set to one.  See Section 3.5 for a 4
description of the fragmentation process. 5
NOTE: As an alternative to radio-transport-level fragmentation, application fragmentation can be implemented.  In this 6
case the application can take the responsibility to ensure all transport messages are not too long (fit within the necessary 7
transport payload size).  When this “application layer fragmentation” is used, the subsequence identifier shall always be 8
set to “0”, and the E-bit set to “1” (See Section 3.5). 9

a) Sequence ID is unique per eAxC (ecpriRtcid or ecpriPcid): different eAxC values have their own Sequence ID 15

b) Sequence ID is generated by the fronthaul interface transmitter in either the O-DU or O-RU 17
c) The sequence generator and checker for a C-Plane message describing U-Plane DL is independent of the 18
sequence generator and checker for a C-Plane message describing U-Plane UL. 19

3.1.3.2.1 RoEsubType (sub type / message type) 9
Description: This field indicates the payload type within the IEEE 1914.3 Standard for Radio over Ethernet 10
Encapsulations and Mappings (RoE) subType range. RoE allows RoE subTypes in the range 128 to 191 to be mapped 11
to external organizations and companies using a subtype mapping table (below). This table has two fields. The 12
OUI/CID field uses a unique Company ID (CID) value. For O-this version of this specification, the OUI/CID assigned 13
by IEEE to xRAN (0xFAEB6E) shall be used.  The payload structure mapping field assigns the RoE subtype to O-RAN 14
message types. When the payload structure mapping field is set to 0xFFFF, this indicates that that RoE subtype is not 15
mapped to an O-RAN message type.  16
A given O-RAN implementation may choose to have fixed (default) mappings, or alternatively, it may choose to 17
configure the subType mapping table using control packets as described in IEEE 1914.3.  18

with IEEE are needed to convert these to O-RAN subtype mapping. 20

Description: This field is the size in bytes of the payload part of the message. The payload length field value is the total 10
number of octets following the O-RAN common header. It does not include the Ethernet FCS or following bytes. 11
Value range: 0 – 0xFFFF. 12

3.2.3 S-plane 19
Frequency and time synchronization of O-DUs and O-RUs via Ethernet use Synchronous Ethernet and IEEE 1588-2008 20
Precision Time Protocol (PTP). Transport of PTP directly over L2 Ethernet (ITU-T G.8275.1 full timing on-path 21
support) is assumed in this version of the specification, whilst transport of PTP over UDP/IP (ITU-T G.8275.2 partial 22
timing support from the network) is also possible albeit with unassured synchronization performance. Security 23
mechanisms will not be used for this plane. The protocol stack for PTP and SyncE over L2 Ethernet is depicted in 24
Figure 3-9. 25
    26
Figure 3-9 : S-plane protocol structure 27

3.3 Quality of Service 29
The LLS interface needs to support the ability to distinguish between data flows with different QoS requirements.   30
Configurable priority levels (via the M-Plane) for traffic prioritization of flows that shall be supported on each node on 31
the network path.  Default values for the respective O-RAN planes are indicated on Table 3-7. Priority marking per 32
packet is needed in each protocol layer except for protocols that do not allow such tagging e.g. ITU -T G.8264 for SyncE 33
and ITU-T G.8275.1 for full timing support PTP. For operation at Layer 2, prioritization is performed by specifying a 34
configurable value for the Priority Code Point (PCP) tag in the IEEE 802.1q VLAN header on the outgoing traffic. 35
